</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-below" style="background:#efefef;">
<a class="image" href="/wiki/File:A_coloured_voting_box.svg"><img alt="icon" class="noviewer thumbborder" data-file-height="160" data-file-width="160" decoding="async" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/01/A_coloured_voting_box.svg/16px-A_coloured_voting_box.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/01/A_coloured_voting_box.svg/24px-A_coloured_voting_box.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/01/A_coloured_voting_box.svg/32px-A_coloured_voting_box.svg.png 2x" width="16"/></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Politics" title="Portal:Politics">Politics portal</a></td></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1063604349">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::before{margin-right:-0.125em;content:"[ "}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar a>span,.mw-parser-output .navbar a>abbr{text-decoration:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Basic_forms_of_government" title="Template:Basic forms of government"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Basic_forms_of_government" title="Template talk:Basic forms of government"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Basic_forms_of_government&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table><p>A <b>dictatorship</b> is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Form_of_government" title="Form of government">form of government</a> characterized by a leader or group of leaders that hold government power with few to no limitations. The leader of a dictatorship is called a <a href="/wiki/Dictator" title="Dictator">dictator</a>. Politics in a dictatorship take place between the dictator, the inner circle, and the opposition, which may be peaceful or violent. Dictatorships can be formed by a <a href="/wiki/Coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="Coup d'état">military coup</a> that overthrows the previous government through force or by a <a href="/wiki/Self-coup" title="Self-coup">self-coup</a> in which elected leaders make their rule permanent. Dictatorships can be classified as <a href="/wiki/Military_dictatorship" title="Military dictatorship">military dictatorships</a>, <a href="/wiki/One-party_state" title="One-party state">one-party dictatorships</a>, personalist dictatorships, or <a href="/wiki/Absolute_monarchy" title="Absolute monarchy">absolute monarchies</a>.
</p><p>The term dictatorship originates from its use in <a href="/wiki/Roman_Republic" title="Roman Republic">Roman Republic</a>. Early military dictatorships developed in the post-classical era, particularly in <a href="/wiki/Shogun" title="Shogun">Shogun</a>-era Japan. Modern dictatorships developed in the 19th century as <a href="/wiki/Caudillo" title="Caudillo"><i>caudillos</i></a> seized power in Latin America. <a href="/wiki/Fascism" title="Fascism">Fascist states</a> and <a href="/wiki/Communist_state" title="Communist state">Communist states</a> emerged in Europe the 1920s and 1930s. Fascism was eradicated in the <a href="/wiki/Aftermath_of_World_War_II" title="Aftermath of World War II">aftermath of World War II</a>, while Communism spread to other continents. Personalist dictatorships in Africa and military dictatorships in Latin America became prominent in the 1960s and 1970s. Many dictatorships fell during the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War_(1985%E2%80%931991)" title="Cold War (1985–1991)">end of the Cold War</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Third_wave_of_democratisation" title="Third wave of democratisation">third wave of democratisation</a>. Many dictatorships still exist, particularly in Africa and Asia.
</p><p>Dictatorships often hold elections to establish legitimacy or to provide incentives for members of the ruling party, but these elections are not competitive for the opposition. Stability in a dictatorship is maintained through coercion, which involves the restriction of information, tracking of political opposition, and acts of violence. Strong opposition groups can result in the collapse of a dictatorship through a <a href="/wiki/Coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="Coup d'état">coup</a> or a <a href="/wiki/Revolution" title="Revolution">revolution</a>.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h2>
<p>The word <i>dictator</i> comes from the <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> language word <i>dictātor</i>, <a href="/wiki/Agent_noun" title="Agent noun">agent noun</a> from <i>dictare (dictāt-</i>, past participial stem of <i>dictāre</i> <a class="external text" href="http://www.oed.com/view/Entry/52299#eid6726598" rel="nofollow">dictate <i>v.</i></a> + <i>-or</i> <a class="external text" href="http://www.oed.com/view/Entry/132131#eid33232093" rel="nofollow">-or <i>suffix</i></a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> In Latin use, a <i>dictator</i> was a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magistratus" title="Magistratus">judge</a> in the Roman Republic temporarily invested with absolute power. Typically, in a dictatorial regime, the leader of the country is identified with the title of dictator; although, their formal title may more closely resemble something similar to <i>leader.</i>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Structure">Structure</span></h2>
<p>The leader of a dictatorship is called a dictator. The power structures of dictatorships vary, and different definitions of dictatorship consider different elements of this structure. Political scientists such as <a href="/wiki/Juan_Jos%C3%A9_Linz" title="Juan José Linz">Juan José Linz</a> and <a href="/wiki/Samuel_P._Huntington" title="Samuel P. Huntington">Samuel P. Huntington</a> identify key attributes that define the power structure of a dictatorship, including a single leader or a small group of leaders, the exercise of power with few limitations, limited <a href="/wiki/Pluralism_(political_theory)" title="Pluralism (political theory)">political pluralism</a>, and limited <a href="/wiki/Mass_mobilization" title="Mass mobilization">mass mobilization</a>. Dictatorship may also be defined as a lack of democracy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEzrowFrantz20112_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEzrowFrantz20112-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p><p>The dictator exercises broad power over the government and society, but other individuals are necessary to carry out the dictator's rule. These individuals form an inner circle, making up a class of elites that hold a degree of power within the dictatorship and receive benefits in exchange for their support. They may be military officers, party members, or friends and family of the dictator. Elites are also the primary political threats of a dictator, as they can leverage their power to influence or overthrow the dictatorship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEzrowFrantz201182–83_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEzrowFrantz201182–83-3">[3]</a></sup> The inner circle's support is necessary for a dictator's orders to be carried out, causing elites to serve as a check on the dictator's power. To enact policy, a dictator must either appease the regime's elites or attempt to replace them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEzrowFrantz2011113–117_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEzrowFrantz2011113–117-4">[4]</a></sup> Elites must also compete to wield more power than one another, but the amount of power held by elites also depends on their unity. Factions or divisions among the elites will mitigate their ability to bargain with the dictator, resulting in the dictator having more unrestrained power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGeddesWrightFrantz201865–66_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGeddesWrightFrantz201865–66-5">[5]</a></sup> A unified inner circle has the capacity to overthrow a dictator, and the dictator must make greater concessions to the inner circle to stay in power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGeddesWrightFrantz201876–79_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGeddesWrightFrantz201876–79-6">[6]</a></sup> This is particularly true when the inner circle is made up of military officers that have the resources to carry out a military coup.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGeddesWrightFrantz201897–99_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGeddesWrightFrantz201897–99-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>The opposition to a dictatorship represents all of the factions that are not part of the dictatorship and anyone that does not support the regime. Organized opposition is a threat to the stability of a dictatorship, as it seeks to undermine public support for the dictator and calls for regime change. A dictator may address the opposition by repressing it through force, modifying laws to restrict its power, or appeasing it with limited benefits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEzrowFrantz201156–57_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEzrowFrantz201156–57-8">[8]</a></sup> The opposition can be an external group, or it can also include current and former members of the dictator's inner circle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGeddesWrightFrantz2018178_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGeddesWrightFrantz2018178-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Totalitarianism" title="Totalitarianism">Totalitarianism</a> is a variation of dictatorship characterized by the presence of a single political party and more specifically, by a powerful leader who imposes personal and political prominence. Power is enforced through a steadfast collaboration between the government and a highly developed ideology. A totalitarian government has "total control of mass communications and social and economic organizations".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> Political philosopher <a href="/wiki/Hannah_Arendt" title="Hannah Arendt">Hannah Arendt</a> describes totalitarianism as a new and extreme form of dictatorship composed of "atomized, isolated individuals" in which ideology plays a leading role in defining how the entire society should be organized. According to Linz, the distinction between an <a href="/wiki/Authoritarianism" title="Authoritarianism">authoritarian</a> regime and a totalitarian one is that an authoritarian regime seeks to suffocate politics and political mobilization while totalitarianism seeks to control and utilize them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Formation">Formation</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CongressBuilding_SEATO.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3223" data-file-width="4851" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/CongressBuilding_SEATO.jpg/220px-CongressBuilding_SEATO.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/CongressBuilding_SEATO.jpg/330px-CongressBuilding_SEATO.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/CongressBuilding_SEATO.jpg/440px-CongressBuilding_SEATO.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CongressBuilding_SEATO.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asia_Treaty_Organization" title="Southeast Asia Treaty Organization">SEATO</a> leaders, including dictators <a href="/wiki/Nguy%E1%BB%85n_Cao_K%E1%BB%B3" title="Nguyễn Cao Kỳ">Nguyen Cao Ky</a> from <a href="/wiki/South_Vietnam" title="South Vietnam">South Vietnam</a> (first from left), <a href="/wiki/Park_Chung-hee" title="Park Chung-hee">Park Chung-Hee</a> from <a href="/wiki/South_Korea" title="South Korea">South Korea</a> (third from left), <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_Marcos" title="Ferdinand Marcos">Ferdinand Marcos</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a> (fourth from the left), and <a href="/wiki/Thanom_Kittikachorn" title="Thanom Kittikachorn">Thanom Kittikachorn</a> from <a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">Thailand</a> (second from the right)</div></div></div>
<p>A dictatorship is formed when a specific group seizes power. The composition of this group affects how power is seized and how the eventual dictatorship will rule. The seizure group may be military or political, it may be organized or disorganized, and it may disproportionately represent a certain demographic. After power is seized, the group must determine what positions its members will hold in the new government and how this government will operate, sometimes resulting in disagreements that split the group. Members of the group will typically make up the elites in a dictator's inner circle at the beginning of a new dictatorship, though the dictator may remove them as a means to gain additional power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGeddesWrightFrantz20183–13_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGeddesWrightFrantz20183–13-12">[12]</a></sup> Unless they have undertaken a self-coup, seizure groups typically have little governmental experience and do not have a detailed policy plan before taking power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGeddesWrightFrantz201837_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGeddesWrightFrantz201837-13">[13]</a></sup> After a dictator seizes power, a political party may be formed to create a mechanism to reward supporters and to concentrate power in the hands of allies instead of armed supporters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGeddesWrightFrantz2018115–118_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGeddesWrightFrantz2018115–118-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p><p>Most dictatorships are formed through military means or through a political party. Nearly half of dictatorships start as a military coup, though others have been started by foreign intervention, elected officials ending competitive elections, insurgent takeovers, popular uprisings by citizens, or a rule-change by autocratic elites to take power within their government. Between 1946 and 2010, 42% of dictatorships began by overthrowing a different dictatorship, and 26% began by declaring independence from a foreign government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGeddesWrightFrantz201826–31_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGeddesWrightFrantz201826–31-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p>Several theories exist as to why dictatorships form. <a href="/wiki/Mancur_Olson" title="Mancur Olson">Mancur Olson</a> suggests that dictatorships are an alternative to "roving bandits"; rather than moving from place to place and extracting wealth, dictators can monopolize banditry in one place while providing an illusion of security to increase productivity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Peter_Alter" title="Peter Alter">Peter Alter</a> suggests a value system perspective based on cultural &amp; political nation, national consciousness, and nation-building.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Types">Types</span></h2>
<p>Political scientist <a href="/wiki/Barbara_Geddes" title="Barbara Geddes">Barbara Geddes</a> describes three types of dictatorship. Military dictatorships are controlled by military officers, single-party dictatorships are controlled by the members of a political party, and personalist dictatorships are controlled by a single individual. Monarchies are also considered dictatorships in some circumstances if they hold significant political power. Hybrid dictatorships are regimes that have a combination of these classifications.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEzrowFrantz201120–22_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEzrowFrantz201120–22-18">[18]</a></sup> Dictatorships can also be defined by the degree of authoritarianism, the use of loyalty or repression, how they interact with the organization that brought them to power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEzrowFrantz20118–14_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEzrowFrantz20118–14-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Military_dictatorships">Military dictatorships</span></h3>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Military_dictatorship" title="Military dictatorship">Military dictatorship</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chan_o_cha_Portrait.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="360" data-file-width="299" decoding="async" height="205" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Chan_o_cha_Portrait.jpg/170px-Chan_o_cha_Portrait.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Chan_o_cha_Portrait.jpg/255px-Chan_o_cha_Portrait.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/58/Chan_o_cha_Portrait.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chan_o_cha_Portrait.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Thailand's Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Prayut_Chan-o-cha" title="Prayut Chan-o-cha">Prayut Chan-o-cha</a> in 2014</div></div></div>
<p>Military dictatorships are regimes in which one or more military officers holds power, determines who will lead the country, and exercises influence over policy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEzrowFrantz201120_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEzrowFrantz201120-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> They are most common in developing nations in Africa, Asia, and Latin America. They are often unstable, and the average duration of a military dictatorship is only five years, but they are often followed by additional military coups and military dictatorships. While common in the 20th century, the prominence of military dictatorships declined in the 1970s and 1980s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p><p>Military dictatorships are typically formed by a military coup in which one or more senior officers uses the military to overthrow the government. In democracies, the threat of a military coup is associated with the period immediately after a democracy's creation but prior to large-scale military reforms. In oligarchies, the threat of a military coup comes from the strength of the military weighed against the concessions made to the military. Other factors associated with military coups include extensive natural resources, limited use of the military internationally, and use of the military as an oppressive force domestically.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> Military coups do not necessarily result in military dictatorships, as power may then be passed to an individual or the military may allow democratic elections to take place. Military dictatorships in the past have been significantly more common in Latin America.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEzrowFrantz201134–38_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEzrowFrantz201134–38-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p><p>Military dictatorships often have traits in common due to the shared background of military dictators. These dictators may view themselves as impartial in their oversight of a country due to their nonpartisan status, and they may view themselves as "guardians of the state". The predominance of violent force in military training manifests in an acceptance of violence as a political tool and the ability to organize violence on a large scale. Military dictators may also be less trusting or diplomatic and underestimate the use of bargaining and compromise in politics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="One-party_dictatorships">One-party dictatorships</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/One-party_state" title="One-party state">One-party state</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Xi_Jinping_2019.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="739" data-file-width="553" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Xi_Jinping_2019.jpg/170px-Xi_Jinping_2019.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Xi_Jinping_2019.jpg/255px-Xi_Jinping_2019.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Xi_Jinping_2019.jpg/340px-Xi_Jinping_2019.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Xi_Jinping_2019.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Xi_Jinping" title="Xi Jinping">Xi Jinping</a>, current <a href="/wiki/General_Secretary_of_the_Chinese_Communist_Party" title="General Secretary of the Chinese Communist Party">CCP General Secretary</a>, ruling <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>One-party dictatorships are governments in which a single political party dominates politics. Single-party dictatorships are one-party states in which only the party in power is legalized and all opposition parties are banned. <a href="/wiki/Dominant-party_system" title="Dominant-party system">Dominant-party dictatorships</a> or electoral authoritarian dictatorships are one-party states in which opposition parties are nominally legal but cannot meaningfully influence government. Single-party dictatorships were more common during the Cold War, but dominant-party dictatorships replaced them after the fall of the Soviet Union.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-:4-26">[26]</a></sup> One-party dictatorships are distinct from political parties that were created to serve a dictator; the ruling party in a one-party dictatorship permeates every level of society. One-party dictatorships became prominent in Asia and Eastern Europe during the Cold War as Communist governments were installed in several countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEzrowFrantz201139–42_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEzrowFrantz201139–42-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p><p>One-party dictatorships are more stable than other forms of authoritarian rule, as they are less susceptible to insurgency and see higher economic growth. Ruling parties allow a dictatorship to more broadly influence the populace and facilitate political agreement between party elites. Between 1950 and 2016, one-party dictatorships made up 57% of authoritarian regimes in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-:4-26">[26]</a></sup> Due to the structure of their leadership, one-party dictatorships are significantly less likely to face civil conflict, insurgency, or terrorism than other forms of dictatorship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> The use of ruling parties also provides more legitimacy to its leadership and elites than other forms of dictatorship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-:5-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p><p>A ruling party in a one-party dictatorship may rule under any ideology or it may have no guiding ideology. One-party states ruled by Marxist political parties are sometimes distinguished from other types of one-party dictatorship, but they are distinct in ideology rather than function.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> When a one-party dictatorship develops gradually through legal means, in can result in conflict between the party organization and the state apparatus and <a href="/wiki/Civil_service" title="Civil service">civil service</a> as the party rules in parallel and increasingly appoints its own members to positions of power. Parties that take power through violence are often able to implement larger changes in a shorter period of time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-:5-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Personalist_dictatorships">Personalist dictatorships</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Alexander_Lukashenko_(2020-09-03)_01(cropped).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="560" data-file-width="409" decoding="async" height="233" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Alexander_Lukashenko_%282020-09-03%29_01%28cropped%29.jpg/170px-Alexander_Lukashenko_%282020-09-03%29_01%28cropped%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Alexander_Lukashenko_%282020-09-03%29_01%28cropped%29.jpg/255px-Alexander_Lukashenko_%282020-09-03%29_01%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Alexander_Lukashenko_%282020-09-03%29_01%28cropped%29.jpg/340px-Alexander_Lukashenko_%282020-09-03%29_01%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Alexander_Lukashenko_(2020-09-03)_01(cropped).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Alexander_Lukashenko" title="Alexander Lukashenko">Alexander Lukashenko</a>, President of Belarus</div></div></div>
<p>Personalist dictatorships are regimes in which all power lies in the hands of a single individual. They differ from other forms of dictatorships in their access to key political positions, other fruits of office, and depend much more on the discretion of the dictator. Personalist dictators may be members of the military or leaders of a political party, but neither the military nor the party exercises power independently from the dictator. In personalist dictatorships, the elite corps are usually made up of close friends or family members of the dictator, who typically handpicks these individuals to serve their posts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEzrowFrantz2011215–216_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEzrowFrantz2011215–216-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> These dictatorships often emerge either from loosely organized seizures of power, giving the leader opportunity to consolidate power, or from democratically-elected leaders in countries with weak institutions, giving the leader opportunity to change the constitution. Personalist dictatorships are more common in Sub-Saharan Africa due to less established institutions in the region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEzrowFrantz201142–45_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEzrowFrantz201142–45-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>Personalist dictators typically favor loyalty over competence in their governments and have a general distrust of intelligentsia. Elites in personalist dictatorships often do not have a professional political career and are unqualified for positions they are given. A personalist dictator will manage these appointees by segmenting the government so that they cannot collaborate. The result is that such regimes have no internal checks and balances, and are thus unrestrained when exerting repression on their people, making radical shifts in foreign policy, or starting wars with other countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> Due to the lack of accountability and the smaller group of elites, personalist dictatorships are more prone to corruption than other forms of dictatorship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEzrowFrantz2011134–135_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEzrowFrantz2011134–135-36">[36]</a></sup> According to a 2019 study, personalist dictatorships are more repressive than other forms of dictatorship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> Personalist dictatorships often collapse with the death of the dictator. They are more likely to end in violence and less likely to democratize than other forms of dictatorship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEzrowFrantz201161–67_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEzrowFrantz201161–67-38">[38]</a></sup>
</p><p>The shift in the power relation between the dictator and its inner circle has severe consequences for the behavior of such regimes as a whole. Many scholars have identified ways in which personalist regimes diverge from other regimes when it comes to their longevity, methods of breakdown, levels of corruption, and proneness to conflicts. Personalist dictatorships on average last twice as long as military dictatorships but not as long as single-party dictatorships.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> Personalist dictatorships also experience growth differently, as they often lack the institutions or qualified leadership to sustain an economy. Without any checks and balances to their rule, such dictators are domestically unopposed when it comes to unleashing repression, or even starting wars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Absolute_monarchies">Absolute monarchies</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Absolute_monarchy" title="Absolute monarchy">Absolute monarchy</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Salman_of_Saudi_Arabia_(2017-10-05)_2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1160" data-file-width="1880" decoding="async" height="136" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Salman_of_Saudi_Arabia_%282017-10-05%29_2.jpg/220px-Salman_of_Saudi_Arabia_%282017-10-05%29_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Salman_of_Saudi_Arabia_%282017-10-05%29_2.jpg/330px-Salman_of_Saudi_Arabia_%282017-10-05%29_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Salman_of_Saudi_Arabia_%282017-10-05%29_2.jpg/440px-Salman_of_Saudi_Arabia_%282017-10-05%29_2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Salman_of_Saudi_Arabia_(2017-10-05)_2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>King <a href="/wiki/Salman_of_Saudi_Arabia" title="Salman of Saudi Arabia">Salman of Saudi Arabia</a></div></div></div><p>An absolute monarchy is a <a href="/wiki/Monarchy" title="Monarchy">monarchy</a> in which the <a href="/wiki/Monarch" title="Monarch">monarch</a> rules without legal limitations. This makes it distinct from <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_monarchy" title="Constitutional monarchy">constitutional monarchy</a> and ceremonial monarchy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEzrowFrantz2011240–241_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEzrowFrantz2011240–241-41">[41]</a></sup> In an absolute monarchy, power is limited to the royal family, and legitimacy is established by historical factors. In the modern era, absolute monarchies are most common in the Middle East.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEzrowFrantz201146–48_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEzrowFrantz201146–48-42">[42]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Montesquieu" title="Montesquieu">Montesquieu</a> made a distinction between despots that ruled unrestrained and monarchs that ruled within the laws of a kingdom. Political parties are relatively rare in monarchic dictatorships compared to military or civilian dictatorships.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> Monarchies may be dynastic, in which the royal family serves as a ruling institution similar to a political party in a one-party state, or they may be non-dynastic, in which the monarch rules independently of the royal family as a personalist dictator.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEzrowFrantz2011259_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEzrowFrantz2011259-44">[44]</a></sup> Monarchies allow for strict rules of succession that produce a peaceful transfer of power on the monarch's death, but this can also result in succession disputes if multiple members of the royal family claim a right to succeed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEzrowFrantz2011254_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEzrowFrantz2011254-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p><h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dictators_in_the_Roman_Empire">Dictators in the Roman Empire</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Roman_dictator" title="Roman dictator">Roman dictator</a></div>
<p>During the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Republic" title="Roman Republic">Republican phase</a> of <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Ancient Rome</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Roman_dictator" title="Roman dictator">Roman dictator</a> was the special <a href="/wiki/Roman_magistrate" title="Roman magistrate">magistrate</a> who held well defined powers, normally for six months at a time, usually in combination with a <a href="/wiki/Roman_consul" title="Roman consul">consulship</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Delirium,_madness_and_crimes_of_Caligula,_the_cruelest_of_the_Roman_emperors_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Delirium,_madness_and_crimes_of_Caligula,_the_cruelest_of_the_Roman_emperors-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Caligula,_the_roman_dictator_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Caligula,_the_roman_dictator-47">[47]</a></sup> These powers were granted during times of crisis in which a single leader was needed to command and restore stability. At least 85 such dictators were chosen over the course of the Roman Republic, the last of which was chosen to wage the <a href="/wiki/Second_Punic_War" title="Second Punic War">Second Punic War</a>. The dictatorship was revived 120 years later through a populist movement led by <a href="/wiki/Sulla" title="Sulla">Sulla</a>, and 33 years after that by <a href="/wiki/Julius_Caesar" title="Julius Caesar">Julius Caesar</a>, under whose dictatorship the Roman Republic became the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-classical_and_early_modern_dictatorships">Post-classical and early modern dictatorships</span></h3>
<p>Japan and Korea saw several military dictatorships during the <a href="/wiki/Post-classical_history" title="Post-classical history">post-classical era</a>. During the early stages of the <a href="/wiki/Goguryeo%E2%80%93Tang_War" title="Goguryeo–Tang War">Goguryeo–Tang War</a> in 642, <a href="/wiki/Yeon_Gaesomun" title="Yeon Gaesomun">Yeon Gaesomun</a> organized a coup in the <a href="/wiki/Goguryeo" title="Goguryeo">Goguryeo</a> Kingdom and established himself as a military dictator.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> A coup took place in the <a href="/wiki/Goryeo" title="Goryeo">Goryeo</a> Kingdom in 1170, establishing the <a href="/wiki/Goryeo_military_regime" title="Goryeo military regime">Goryeo military regime</a> for the next century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Shogun" title="Shogun">Shogun</a> was the title of the <a href="/wiki/Military_dictatorship" title="Military dictatorship">military dictators</a> of Japan beginning with the <a href="/wiki/Kamakura_shogunate" title="Kamakura shogunate">Kamakura shogunate</a> in 1185 and continuing through other shogunates for over six hundred years. While Shoguns nominally operated under the control of the <a href="/wiki/Emperor_of_Japan" title="Emperor of Japan">Emperor of Japan</a>, they served as <i>de facto</i> rulers of Japan and the Japanese military.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_England" title="Commonwealth of England">Commonwealth of England</a> under <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Cromwell" title="Oliver Cromwell">Oliver Cromwell</a>, formed in 1649 after the <a href="/wiki/Second_English_Civil_War" title="Second English Civil War">Second English Civil War</a>, has been described as a military dictatorship by its contemporary opponents and by some modern academics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-54">[54]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Maximilien_Robespierre" title="Maximilien Robespierre">Maximilien Robespierre</a> has been similarly described as a military dictator while he controlled the <a href="/wiki/National_Convention" title="National Convention">National Convention</a> in France and carried out the <a href="/wiki/Reign_of_Terror" title="Reign of Terror">Reign of Terror</a> in 1793 and 1794.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="19th_century_dictatorships">19th century dictatorships</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Santaanna1.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="388" decoding="async" height="219" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Santaanna1.JPG/170px-Santaanna1.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Santaanna1.JPG/255px-Santaanna1.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Santaanna1.JPG/340px-Santaanna1.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Santaanna1.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Antonio_L%C3%B3pez_de_Santa_Anna" title="Antonio López de Santa Anna">Antonio López de Santa Anna</a> wearing a Mexican military uniform</div></div></div>
<p>Dictatorship developed as a major form of government in the 19th century, though in Europe it was often thought of in terms of <a href="/wiki/Bonapartism" title="Bonapartism">Bonapartism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Caesarism" title="Caesarism">Caesarism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> The concept of dictatorship was not universally seen pejoratively at the time, with both a tyrannical concept and a quasi-constitutional concept of dictatorship understood to exist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Spanish_American_wars_of_independence" title="Spanish American wars of independence">Spanish American wars of independence</a> took place in the early-19th century, creating many new <a href="/wiki/Latin_America" title="Latin America">Latin American</a> governments. Many of these governments fell under the control of <i>caudillos</i>, or personalist strongmen dictators. Most caudillos came from a military background, and their rule was typically associated with pageantry and glamor. Caudillos were often nominally constrained by a constitution, but the caudillo had the power to draft a new constitution as he wished. Many are noted for their cruelty, while others are honored as national heroes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p><p>Many Latin American countries experienced dictatorships by caudillos shortly after their formation in the early-19th century. <a href="/wiki/Juan_Manuel_de_Rosas" title="Juan Manuel de Rosas">Juan Manuel de Rosas</a> was a major figure in the formation of Argentina, ruling as dictator from 1829 to 1852. In Guatemala, <a href="/wiki/Rafael_Carrera" title="Rafael Carrera">Rafael Carrera</a> ruled as dictator from 1839 to 1865. In Venezuela, <a href="/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_Antonio_P%C3%A1ez" title="José Antonio Páez">José Antonio Páez</a> ruled in the decades following the country's founding. <a href="/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_Gaspar_Rodr%C3%ADguez_de_Francia" title="José Gaspar Rodríguez de Francia">José Gaspar Rodríguez de Francia</a> ruled Paraguay from its founding until 1840.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-60">[60]</a></sup> President <a href="/wiki/Antonio_L%C3%B3pez_de_Santa_Anna" title="Antonio López de Santa Anna">Antonio López de Santa Anna</a> of Mexico established himself as a military dictator, and he gave himself the title of His Serene Highness in 1853. He was <a href="/wiki/Plan_of_Ayutla" title="Plan of Ayutla">overthrown</a> and exiled in 1855.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> President <a href="/wiki/Porfirio_D%C3%ADaz" title="Porfirio Díaz">Porfirio Díaz</a> of Mexico <a href="/wiki/Plan_of_Tuxtepec" title="Plan of Tuxtepec">took power</a> in 1876 and served as the military dictator until the <a href="/wiki/Mexican_Revolution" title="Mexican Revolution">Mexican Revolution</a> in a period known as the <a href="/wiki/Porfiriato" title="Porfiriato">Porfiriato</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pre-Cold_War_dictatorships_in_the_20th_century">Pre-Cold War dictatorships in the 20th century <span class="anchor" id="Dictatorships_of_the_World_Wars"></span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Europe">Europe <span class="anchor" id="European_dictatorships_of_the_World_Wars"></span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/European_interwar_dictatorships" title="European interwar dictatorships">European interwar dictatorships</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hitlermusso2_edit.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1100" data-file-width="697" decoding="async" height="268" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b1/Hitlermusso2_edit.jpg/170px-Hitlermusso2_edit.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b1/Hitlermusso2_edit.jpg/255px-Hitlermusso2_edit.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b1/Hitlermusso2_edit.jpg/340px-Hitlermusso2_edit.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hitlermusso2_edit.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Benito_Mussolini" title="Benito Mussolini">Benito Mussolini</a> (left) and <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Hitler" title="Adolf Hitler">Adolf Hitler</a> (right)</div></div></div>
<p>Dictatorship expanded in early-20th century Europe through a combination of far-left and far-right takeovers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELee20161_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELee20161-63">[63]</a></sup> The aftermath of <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a> resulted in a major shift in European government, establishing new governments, facilitating internal change in other governments, and redrawing the boundaries between countries. These changes provided opportunities for extremist far-left and far-right political movements to take power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELee20165_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELee20165-64">[64]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin" title="Vladimir Lenin">Vladimir Lenin</a> established <a href="/wiki/Russian_Soviet_Federative_Socialist_Republic" title="Russian Soviet Federative Socialist Republic">Soviet Russia</a> in 1917 as a <a href="/wiki/Dictatorship_of_the_proletariat" title="Dictatorship of the proletariat">dictatorship of the proletariat</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELee201636_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELee201636-65">[65]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Bolsheviks" title="Bolsheviks">Bolshevik</a> government during the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Civil_War" title="Russian Civil War">Russian Civil War</a> had democratic elements, but following poor electoral performance, Lenin dissolved the Constituent Assembly. The <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> was formed as a dictatorship in 1922 under the control of Lenin and the other Bolsheviks, whose power was enforced through secret police organizations such as the <a href="/wiki/Cheka" title="Cheka">Cheka</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELee201648–50_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELee201648–50-66">[66]</a></sup> Following Lenin's death in 1924, the party chose <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Stalin" title="Joseph Stalin">Joseph Stalin</a> to be its next leader, and Stalin gained total control by 1929.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELee201655_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELee201655-67">[67]</a></sup> In the following decades, Stalin carried out a <a href="/wiki/Totalitarianism" title="Totalitarianism">totalitarian</a> rule in which all threats to his power were eliminated through <a href="/wiki/Purges_of_the_Communist_Party_of_the_Soviet_Union" title="Purges of the Communist Party of the Soviet Union">purges</a>, eliminating democracy even within the one party system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELee201659–60_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELee201659–60-68">[68]</a></sup> A Soviet government was briefly established in <a href="/wiki/Hungarian_Soviet_Republic" title="Hungarian Soviet Republic">Hungary</a> in 1919, but it fell the same year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELee2016322_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELee2016322-69">[69]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Benito_Mussolini" title="Benito Mussolini">Benito Mussolini</a> was the first fascist dictator. He carried out the <a href="/wiki/March_on_Rome" title="March on Rome">March on Rome</a> in 1922, forcing King <a href="/wiki/Victor_Emmanuel_III_of_Italy" title="Victor Emmanuel III of Italy">Victor Emmanuel III</a> to appoint him as <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_Italy" title="Prime Minister of Italy">Prime Minister of Italy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELee2016114–115_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELee2016114–115-70">[70]</a></sup> In 1925, he began implementing his system of fascism, based on totalitarianism, fealty to the state, <a href="/wiki/Expansionism" title="Expansionism">expansionism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Corporatism" title="Corporatism">corporatism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Anti-communism" title="Anti-communism">anti-communism</a>, though its application varied and was often inconsistent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELee2016122–124_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELee2016122–124-71">[71]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Hitler" title="Adolf Hitler">Adolf Hitler</a> was appointed chancellor of the <a href="/wiki/Weimar_Republic" title="Weimar Republic">Weimar Republic</a> in 1933, his <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Hitler%27s_rise_to_power" title="Adolf Hitler's rise to power">rise to power</a> coinciding with weakening democracy in the country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELee2016178–179_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELee2016178–179-72">[72]</a></sup> Over the following six months, Hitler and the <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Party" title="Nazi Party">Nazi Party</a> obtained absolute power through a combination of electoral victory, violence, and emergency powers, assuming the office of president in addition to chancellor and taking the title of <a href="/wiki/F%C3%BChrer" title="Führer">Führer</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELee2016186–188_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELee2016186–188-73">[73]</a></sup> Austria briefly underwent a <a href="/wiki/Federal_State_of_Austria" title="Federal State of Austria">period of dictatorship</a> under <a href="/wiki/Engelbert_Dollfuss" title="Engelbert Dollfuss">Engelbert Dollfuss</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kurt_Schuschnigg" title="Kurt Schuschnigg">Kurt Schuschnigg</a> before being <a href="/wiki/Anschluss" title="Anschluss">annexed</a> by Germany.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELee2016318_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELee2016318-74">[74]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Miguel_Primo_de_Rivera" title="Miguel Primo de Rivera">Miguel Primo de Rivera</a> was appointed <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_Spain" title="Prime Minister of Spain">Prime Minister of Spain</a> in 1923 following a military coup, and he ruled as a dictator. By 1930, Primo de Rivera had alienated every major faction of Spanish politics and was convinced to step down. His government was replaced by the <a href="/wiki/Second_Spanish_Republic" title="Second Spanish Republic">Second Spanish Republic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELee2016266–269_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELee2016266–269-75">[75]</a></sup> In 1926, <a href="/wiki/Manuel_Gomes_da_Costa" title="Manuel Gomes da Costa">Manuel Gomes da Costa</a> led a military coup and established the <i><a href="/wiki/Ditadura_Nacional" title="Ditadura Nacional">Ditadura Nacional</a></i> in Portugal. His successor, <a href="/wiki/%C3%93scar_Carmona" title="Óscar Carmona">Óscar Carmona</a>, chose to give control of finances to economics professor <a href="/wiki/Ant%C3%B3nio_de_Oliveira_Salazar" title="António de Oliveira Salazar">António de Oliveira Salazar</a>. Salazar became <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_Portugal" title="Prime Minister of Portugal">Prime Minister of Portugal</a> in 1932 and established the <a href="/wiki/Estado_Novo_(Portugal)" title="Estado Novo (Portugal)"><i>Estado Novo</i></a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELee2016305–306_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELee2016305–306-76">[76]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Francisco_Franco" title="Francisco Franco">Francisco Franco</a> took power in Spain after leading the <a href="/wiki/Nationalist_faction_(Spanish_Civil_War)" title="Nationalist faction (Spanish Civil War)">Nationalist faction</a> to victory in the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Civil_War" title="Spanish Civil War">Spanish Civil War</a> and became a dictator in 1939.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELee2016297–298_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELee2016297–298-77">[77]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Baltic_states" title="Baltic states">Baltic states</a> all became dictatorships between the World Wars. <a href="/wiki/Antanas_Smetona" title="Antanas Smetona">Antanas Smetona</a> took power in Lithuania in 1926, <a href="/wiki/Konstantin_P%C3%A4ts" title="Konstantin Päts">Konstantin Päts</a> took power in Estonia in 1934, and <a href="/wiki/K%C4%81rlis_Ulmanis" title="Kārlis Ulmanis">Kārlis Ulmanis</a> took power in Latvia in 1934. The dictatorships in the Baltic states were considered to be milder than those in other European countries, with private enterprise and the press being relatively free. These dictatorships ended after they were <a href="/wiki/Occupation_of_the_Baltic_states" title="Occupation of the Baltic states">occupied</a> in World War II.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELee2016333–334_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELee2016333–334-78">[78]</a></sup>
</p><p>Several <a href="/wiki/Right-wing_dictatorship" title="Right-wing dictatorship">right-wing dictatorships</a> also emerged in the <a href="/wiki/Balkans" title="Balkans">Balkans</a> during the interwar period. Bulgaria underwent a period of several dictatorships following a military coup in 1923.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELee2016341–342_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELee2016341–342-79">[79]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ahmed_Zogu" title="Ahmed Zogu">Ahmed Zogu</a> overthrew the government in Albania in 1924, and he proclaimed himself King Zog I in 1928. He was dictator until Albania was occupied in World War II.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELee2016338_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELee2016338-80">[80]</a></sup> King <a href="/wiki/George_II_of_Greece" title="George II of Greece">George II of Greece</a> appointed <a href="/wiki/Ioannis_Metaxas" title="Ioannis Metaxas">Ioannis Metaxas</a> as <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_Greece" title="Prime Minister of Greece">Prime Minister of Greece</a> in 1936, and Metaxas ruled as a dictator until his death in 1941.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELee2016352_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELee2016352-81">[81]</a></sup> In 1940, King <a href="/wiki/Michael_I_of_Romania" title="Michael I of Romania">Michael I of Romania</a> granted <a href="/wiki/Ion_Antonescu" title="Ion Antonescu">Ion Antonescu</a> dictatorial powers, and Antonescu declared himself the <a href="/wiki/Conduc%C4%83tor" title="Conducător">Conducător</a> of Romania.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELee2016349–350_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELee2016349–350-82">[82]</a></sup>
</p><p>During <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, Germany and Italy <a href="/wiki/German-occupied_Europe" title="German-occupied Europe">occupied</a> several European countries and established fascist client states. These include <a href="/wiki/Italian_protectorate_of_Albania_(1939%E2%80%931943)" title="Italian protectorate of Albania (1939–1943)">Albania</a>, <a href="/wiki/German_occupation_of_Belgium_during_World_War_II" title="German occupation of Belgium during World War II">Belgium</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Protectorate_of_Bohemia_and_Moravia" title="Protectorate of Bohemia and Moravia">Czech lands</a>, <a href="/wiki/Denmark_in_World_War_II" title="Denmark in World War II">Denmark</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vichy_France" title="Vichy France">France</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hellenic_State_(1941%E2%80%931944)" title="Hellenic State (1941–1944)">Greece</a>, <a href="/wiki/Government_of_National_Unity_(Hungary)" title="Government of National Unity (Hungary)">Hungary</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands_in_World_War_II" title="Netherlands in World War II">Netherlands</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_Poland_(1939-1945)" title="History of Poland (1939-1945)">Poland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Quisling_regime" title="Quisling regime">Norway</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Slovak_Republic_(1939%E2%80%931945)" title="Slovak Republic (1939–1945)">Slovak Republic</a>, <a href="/wiki/World_War_II_in_Yugoslavia" title="World War II in Yugoslavia">Yugoslavia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union_in_World_War_II" title="Soviet Union in World War II">The Soviet Union</a>. Poland was <a href="/wiki/Occupation_of_Poland_(1939%E2%80%931945)" title="Occupation of Poland (1939–1945)">occupied</a> by both Germany and the Soviet Union. <a href="/wiki/End_of_World_War_II_in_Europe" title="End of World War II in Europe">Allied victory in Europe</a> and the fall of Nazi Germany resulted in the liberation of Nazi-occupied territories and the dissolving of fascist governments.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Latin_America">Latin America <span class="anchor" id="Latin_American_dictatorships_of_the_World_Wars"></span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Augusto_B._Legu%C3%ADa" title="Augusto B. Leguía">Augusto B. Leguía</a> ruled as a dictator in Peru from 1919 to 1930.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Juan_Vicente_G%C3%B3mez" title="Juan Vicente Gómez">Juan Vicente Gómez</a> ruled Venezuela as a dictator from 1908 until his death in 1935. <a href="/wiki/Rafael_Trujillo" title="Rafael Trujillo">Rafael Trujillo</a> took power as a "nearly totalitarian" dictator of the Dominican Republic in the 1930s and ruled for 30 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-60">[60]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/1930_Argentine_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="1930 Argentine coup d'état">1930 Argentine coup d'état</a> was the first in a series of coups and military dictatorships that would affect the country for decades.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Jorge_Ubico" title="Jorge Ubico">Jorge Ubico</a> took power in Guatemala in 1931 and ruled until he was deposed by a pro-democracy uprising in 1944.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> In 1931, a coup was organized against the government of <a href="/wiki/Arturo_Araujo" title="Arturo Araujo">Arturo Araujo</a>, causing the period of <a href="/wiki/Military_dictatorship_in_El_Salvador" title="Military dictatorship in El Salvador">military dictatorship in El Salvador</a>, starting with the <a href="/wiki/Civic_Directory" title="Civic Directory">Civic Directory</a>. The government committed several crimes against humanity, such as those of <a href="/wiki/La_Matanza" title="La Matanza">La Matanza</a>. The dictatorship ended with the <a href="/wiki/1979_Salvadoran_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="1979 Salvadoran coup d'état">1979 Salvadoran coup d'état</a> and the start of the <a href="/wiki/Salvadoran_Civil_War" title="Salvadoran Civil War">Salvadoran Civil War</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-beverley_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-beverley-86">[86]</a></sup> President <a href="/wiki/Tiburcio_Car%C3%ADas_Andino" title="Tiburcio Carías Andino">Tiburcio Carías Andino</a> carried out a self-coup in Honduras after the election of 1932 and served as a dictator until 1949.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> A self-coup in Uruguay established <a href="/wiki/Gabriel_Terra" title="Gabriel Terra">Gabriel Terra</a> as dictator in 1933.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Get%C3%BAlio_Vargas" title="Getúlio Vargas">Getúlio Vargas</a> led a coup in 1937 that established him as a dictator in Brazil under an <i><a href="/wiki/Vargas_Era" title="Vargas Era">Estado Novo</a></i> system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> In Paraguay, <a href="/wiki/Higinio_Mor%C3%ADnigo" title="Higinio Morínigo">Higinio Morínigo</a> established himself as a military dictator in 1940 and ruled until 1948.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cold_War_dictatorships">Cold War dictatorships <span class="anchor" id="Dictatorships_of_the_Cold_War"></span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Africa">Africa <span class="anchor" id="African_dictatorships_of_the_Cold_War"></span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Prins_Bernhard_in_Zaire_(voorheen_Belgisch_Congo),_Bernhard_en_Mobutu,_Bestanddeelnr_926-6037.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2483" data-file-width="2525" decoding="async" height="216" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Prins_Bernhard_in_Zaire_%28voorheen_Belgisch_Congo%29%2C_Bernhard_en_Mobutu%2C_Bestanddeelnr_926-6037.jpg/220px-Prins_Bernhard_in_Zaire_%28voorheen_Belgisch_Congo%29%2C_Bernhard_en_Mobutu%2C_Bestanddeelnr_926-6037.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Prins_Bernhard_in_Zaire_%28voorheen_Belgisch_Congo%29%2C_Bernhard_en_Mobutu%2C_Bestanddeelnr_926-6037.jpg/330px-Prins_Bernhard_in_Zaire_%28voorheen_Belgisch_Congo%29%2C_Bernhard_en_Mobutu%2C_Bestanddeelnr_926-6037.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Prins_Bernhard_in_Zaire_%28voorheen_Belgisch_Congo%29%2C_Bernhard_en_Mobutu%2C_Bestanddeelnr_926-6037.jpg/440px-Prins_Bernhard_in_Zaire_%28voorheen_Belgisch_Congo%29%2C_Bernhard_en_Mobutu%2C_Bestanddeelnr_926-6037.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Prins_Bernhard_in_Zaire_(voorheen_Belgisch_Congo),_Bernhard_en_Mobutu,_Bestanddeelnr_926-6037.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Mobutu_Sese_Seko" title="Mobutu Sese Seko">Mobutu Sese Seko</a>, Zaire's longtime dictator</div></div></div>
<p>Many dictatorships formed in Africa, with most forming after countries gained independence during <a href="/wiki/Decolonisation_of_Africa" title="Decolonisation of Africa">decolonisation</a>. <a href="/wiki/Mobutu_Sese_Seko" title="Mobutu Sese Seko">Mobutu Sese Seko</a> ruled the Democratic Republic of the Congo as a dictator for decades, renaming it <a href="/wiki/Zaire" title="Zaire">Zaire</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Teodoro_Obiang_Nguema_Mbasogo" title="Teodoro Obiang Nguema Mbasogo">Teodoro Obiang Nguema Mbasogo</a> has ruled Equatorial Guinea as a dictator since he led a military coup in 1979.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:03_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-:03-92">[92]</a></sup> In 1973, King <a href="/wiki/Sobhuza_II" title="Sobhuza II">Sobhuza II</a> of Swaziland suspended the constitution and ruled as an absolute monarch.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Doe" title="Samuel Doe">Samuel Doe</a> established a military dictatorship in Liberia in the 1980s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> Libya was ruled by <a href="/wiki/Muammar_Gaddafi" title="Muammar Gaddafi">Muammar Gaddafi</a> for several decades following <a href="/wiki/1969_Libyan_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="1969 Libyan coup d'état">a military coup</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Moussa_Traor%C3%A9" title="Moussa Traoré">Moussa Traoré</a> ruled as a dictator in Mali.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Habib_Bourguiba" title="Habib Bourguiba">Habib Bourguiba</a> ruled as a dictator in Tunisia until he was deposed by a coup led by <a href="/wiki/Zine_El_Abidine_Ben_Ali" title="Zine El Abidine Ben Ali">Zine El Abidine Ben Ali</a> in 1987, who in turn ruled as a dictator until the <a href="/wiki/Tunisian_Revolution" title="Tunisian Revolution">Tunisian Revolution</a> in 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Robert_Mugabe" title="Robert Mugabe">Robert Mugabe</a> ruled as a dictator in Zimbabwe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup>
</p><p>Early socialist dictatorships in Africa mainly developed as personalist dictatorships, in which a single socialist would take power instead of a ruling party. Later in the Cold War, the Soviet Union increased its influence in Africa as Marxist-Leninist dictatorships developed in several African countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> One-party Marxist states in Africa included <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Republic_of_Angola" title="People's Republic of Angola">Angola</a> under the <a href="/wiki/MPLA" title="MPLA">MPLA</a>, <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Republic_of_Benin" title="People's Republic of Benin">Benin</a> under <a href="/wiki/Mathieu_K%C3%A9r%C3%A9kou" title="Mathieu Kérékou">Mathieu Kérékou</a>, Cape Verde under the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PAICV" title="PAICV">PAICV</a>, the <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Republic_of_the_Congo" title="People's Republic of the Congo">Congo</a> under the <a href="/wiki/Congolese_Party_of_Labour" title="Congolese Party of Labour">Congolese Party of Labour</a>, <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Democratic_Republic_of_Ethiopia" title="People's Democratic Republic of Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a> under the <a href="/wiki/Workers%27_Party_of_Ethiopia" title="Workers' Party of Ethiopia">Workers' Party of Ethiopia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Republic_of_Madagascar" title="Democratic Republic of Madagascar">Madagascar</a> under <a href="/wiki/Malagasy_Revolutionary_Party" title="Malagasy Revolutionary Party">AREMA</a>, <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Republic_of_Mozambique" title="People's Republic of Mozambique">Mozambique</a> under <a href="/wiki/FRELIMO" title="FRELIMO">FRELIMO</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Somali_Democratic_Republic" title="Somali Democratic Republic">Somalia</a> under <a href="/wiki/Siad_Barre" title="Siad Barre">Siad Barre</a>.
</p><p>Many African countries underwent several military coups that installed a series of military dictatorships throughout the Cold War. These include Benin, Burkina Faso, Burundi, the Central African Republic, Comoros, the Democratic Republic of the Congo, Ethiopia, Ghana, Mauritania, Niger, Nigeria, Sierra Leone, Sudan, and Uganda, each undergoing at least three successful military coups between 1959 and 2001.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some leaders of African countries abolished opposition parties, establishing one-party dictatorships. These include the <a href="/wiki/National_Liberation_Front_(Algeria)" title="National Liberation Front (Algeria)">National Liberation Front</a> in Algeria,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Chadian_Progressive_Party" title="Chadian Progressive Party">Chadian Progressive Party</a> under <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Tombalbaye" title="François Tombalbaye">François Tombalbaye</a> in Chad,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Gabonese_Democratic_Party" title="Gabonese Democratic Party">Gabonese Democratic Party</a> under <a href="/wiki/Omar_Bongo" title="Omar Bongo">Omar Bongo</a> in Gabon,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_of_Guinea_%E2%80%93_African_Democratic_Rally" title="Democratic Party of Guinea – African Democratic Rally">Democratic Party</a> under <a href="/wiki/Ahmed_S%C3%A9kou_Tour%C3%A9" title="Ahmed Sékou Touré">Ahmed Sékou Touré</a> in Guinea,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Malawi_Congress_Party" title="Malawi Congress Party">Malawi Congress Party</a> under <a href="/wiki/Hastings_Banda" title="Hastings Banda">Hastings Banda</a> in Malawi,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/National_Movement_for_the_Development_of_Society" title="National Movement for the Development of Society">MNSD</a> under <a href="/wiki/Ali_Saibou" title="Ali Saibou">Ali Saibou</a> in Niger,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/National_Revolutionary_Movement_for_Development" title="National Revolutionary Movement for Development">MRND</a> under <a href="/wiki/Juv%C3%A9nal_Habyarimana" title="Juvénal Habyarimana">Juvénal Habyarimana</a> in Rwanda,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Socialist_Party_of_Senegal" title="Socialist Party of Senegal">Socialist Party</a> under <a href="/wiki/L%C3%A9opold_S%C3%A9dar_Senghor" title="Léopold Sédar Senghor">Léopold Sédar Senghor</a> in Senegal,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Julius_Nyerere" title="Julius Nyerere">Julius Nyerere</a> in Tanzania, the <a href="/wiki/Rally_of_the_Togolese_People" title="Rally of the Togolese People">RPT</a> under <a href="/wiki/Gnassingb%C3%A9_Eyad%C3%A9ma" title="Gnassingbé Eyadéma">Gnassingbé Eyadéma</a> in Togo,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/United_National_Independence_Party" title="United National Independence Party">United National Independence Party</a> under <a href="/wiki/Kenneth_Kaunda" title="Kenneth Kaunda">Kenneth Kaunda</a> in Zambia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Kenya_African_National_Union" title="Kenya African National Union">KANU</a> in Kenya ruled under a <i>de facto</i> one-party state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Asia">Asia <span class="anchor" id="Asian_dictatorships_of_the_Cold_War"></span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mao_Zedong_1959_(cropped).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="604" decoding="async" height="291" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Mao_Zedong_1959_%28cropped%29.jpg/220px-Mao_Zedong_1959_%28cropped%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Mao_Zedong_1959_%28cropped%29.jpg/330px-Mao_Zedong_1959_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Mao_Zedong_1959_%28cropped%29.jpg/440px-Mao_Zedong_1959_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mao_Zedong_1959_(cropped).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Mao_Zedong" title="Mao Zedong">Mao Zedong</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chairman_of_the_Chinese_Communist_Party" title="Chairman of the Chinese Communist Party">Chairman</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Communist_Party" title="Chinese Communist Party">Chinese Communist Party</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ali_Khamenei_crop.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1660" data-file-width="1266" decoding="async" height="288" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Ali_Khamenei_crop.jpg/220px-Ali_Khamenei_crop.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Ali_Khamenei_crop.jpg/330px-Ali_Khamenei_crop.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Ali_Khamenei_crop.jpg/440px-Ali_Khamenei_crop.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ali_Khamenei_crop.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Khamenei" title="Khamenei">Khamenei</a>, the dictator of the <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Islamic Republic</a></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Mao_Zedong" title="Mao Zedong">Mao Zedong</a> established the People's Republic of China as a one-party Communist state at the end of the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Civil_War" title="Chinese Civil War">Chinese Civil War</a> in 1949, removing the Nationalist Chinese government from power. Mao implemented his governing ideology of <a href="/wiki/Maoism" title="Maoism">Maoism</a>. The country maintained a strained relationship with the Soviet Union, and relations <a href="/wiki/Sino-Soviet_split" title="Sino-Soviet split">deteriorated</a> as the Soviet Union underwent <a href="/wiki/De-Stalinization" title="De-Stalinization">de-Stalinization</a> in the late-1950s. In the 1960s, amid fear that he would lose control of the Communist Party, Mao initiated the <a href="/wiki/Cultural_Revolution" title="Cultural Revolution">Cultural Revolution</a>, which involved the destruction of any elements of capitalism in China while also establishing relations with Japan and the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Deng_Xiaoping" title="Deng Xiaoping">Deng Xiaoping</a> took power as the <i>de facto</i> leader of China after Mao's death and implemented <a href="/wiki/Chinese_economic_reform" title="Chinese economic reform">reforms</a> to restore stability following the Cultural Revolution and reestablish <a href="/wiki/Free_market" title="Free market">free market</a> economics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Chiang_Kai-shek" title="Chiang Kai-shek">Chiang Kai-shek</a> continued to rule as dictator of the National government's <a href="/wiki/Rump_state" title="Rump state">rump state</a> in <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a> until his death in 1975.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup>
</p><p>North Korea and South Korea both emerged from World War II as dictatorships. North Korea was established under Soviet influence, and <a href="/wiki/Kim_Il-sung" title="Kim Il-sung">Kim Il-sung</a> led the country as a one-party Marxist state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> South Korea was under the dictatorship of <a href="/wiki/Syngman_Rhee" title="Syngman Rhee">Syngman Rhee</a> in the 1950s, but his rule ended following the <a href="/wiki/April_Revolution" title="April Revolution">April Revolution</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> The country became a military dictatorship under <a href="/wiki/Chun_Doo-hwan" title="Chun Doo-hwan">Chun Doo-hwan</a> in the 1980s, but the <a href="/wiki/June_Democratic_Struggle" title="June Democratic Struggle">June Democratic Struggle</a> in 1987 led to democratization.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> In Southern Asia, <a href="/wiki/Ayub_Khan_(general)" title="Ayub Khan (general)">Ayub Khan</a> established himself as a military dictator of Pakistan during the <a href="/wiki/1958_Pakistani_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="1958 Pakistani coup d'état">1958 coup</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Zia-ul-Haq" title="Muhammad Zia-ul-Haq">Muhammad Zia-ul-Haq</a> established himself as a military dictator in the <a href="/wiki/Operation_Fair_Play" title="Operation Fair Play">1977 coup</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hussain_Muhammad_Ershad" title="Hussain Muhammad Ershad">Hussain Muhammad Ershad</a> took power in Bangladesh in a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1982_Bangladesh_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="1982 Bangladesh coup d'état">1982 military coup</a> and ruled until 1991.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> Nepal was an absolute monarchy until the <a href="/wiki/1990_Nepalese_revolution" title="1990 Nepalese revolution">1990 Nepalese revolution</a> created a constitutional monarchy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup>
</p><p>Southeast Asia was influenced by China during the Cold War, and three Communist dictatorships were formed in the region: <a href="/wiki/North_Vietnam" title="North Vietnam">North Vietnam</a>, Laos, and <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Kampuchea" title="Democratic Kampuchea">Kampuchea</a>. North Vietnam conquered <a href="/wiki/South_Vietnam" title="South Vietnam">South Vietnam</a> at the end of the <a href="/wiki/Vietnam_War" title="Vietnam War">Vietnam War</a>, and the two merged into a single Communist country. Anti-Communist dictators also ruled in the region. <a href="/wiki/Suharto" title="Suharto">Suharto</a> became dictator in Indonesia, <a href="/wiki/Transition_to_the_New_Order" title="Transition to the New Order">taking power</a> in 1967 until a <a href="/wiki/May_1998_riots_of_Indonesia" title="May 1998 riots of Indonesia">violent unrest in 1998</a> forced him to <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_Suharto" title="Fall of Suharto">resign</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ngo_Dinh_Diem" title="Ngo Dinh Diem">Ngo Dinh Diem</a> ruled South Vietnam as a dictator until the <a href="/wiki/1963_South_Vietnamese_coup" title="1963 South Vietnamese coup">1963 military coup</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_Marcos" title="Ferdinand Marcos">Ferdinand Marcos</a> ruled Philippines as a dictator until the <a href="/wiki/People_Power_Revolution" title="People Power Revolution">People Power Revolution</a> in 1986.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Socialist_Republic_of_the_Union_of_Burma" title="Socialist Republic of the Union of Burma">socialist military dictatorship</a> was also created separately from the Communist governments in Burma until it was <a href="/wiki/8888_Uprising" title="8888 Uprising">overthrown</a> in 1988 and replaced by a <a href="/wiki/State_Peace_and_Development_Council" title="State Peace and Development Council">new military dictatorship</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Middle East was decolonized during the Cold War, and many nationalist movements gained strength post-independence. A <a href="/wiki/1953_Iranian_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="1953 Iranian coup d'état">1953 coup</a> overseen by the American and British governments restored <a href="/wiki/Mohammad_Reza_Pahlavi" title="Mohammad Reza Pahlavi">Mohammad Reza Pahlavi</a> as the absolute monarch of Iran, who in turn was overthrown during the <a href="/wiki/Iranian_Revolution" title="Iranian Revolution">Iranian Revolution</a> of 1979 that established <a href="/wiki/Ruhollah_Khomeini" title="Ruhollah Khomeini">Ruhollah Khomeini</a> as <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Leader_of_Iran" title="Supreme Leader of Iran">Supreme Leader of Iran</a> under an <a href="/wiki/Islamism" title="Islamism">Islamist</a> government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> Several Middle Eastern countries were the subject of military coups in the 1950s and 1960s, including Iraq, Syria, North Yemen, and South Yemen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> Islamic Republic of Iran is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Totalitarian" title="Totalitarian">totalitarian</a> regime based on <a href="/wiki/Theocracy" title="Theocracy">theocracy</a> ruled with the absolute power of the supreme leader, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Khamenei" title="Khamenei">Khamenei</a>. Afghanistan became a one-party dictatorship after the <a href="/wiki/1973_Afghan_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="1973 Afghan coup d'état">1973 coup</a>, and it became a Marxist dictatorship after the <a href="/wiki/Saur_Revolution" title="Saur Revolution">Saur Revolution</a> in 1978.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Europe_2">Europe <span class="anchor" id="European_dictatorships_of_the_Cold_War"></span></span></h4>
