<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:HOD%C5%BDA_druh%C3%A1_m%C3%ADza.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="441" data-file-width="441" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/HOD%C5%BDA_druh%C3%A1_m%C3%ADza.jpg/220px-HOD%C5%BDA_druh%C3%A1_m%C3%ADza.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/HOD%C5%BDA_druh%C3%A1_m%C3%ADza.jpg/330px-HOD%C5%BDA_druh%C3%A1_m%C3%ADza.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/HOD%C5%BDA_druh%C3%A1_m%C3%ADza.jpg/440px-HOD%C5%BDA_druh%C3%A1_m%C3%ADza.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:HOD%C5%BDA_druh%C3%A1_m%C3%ADza.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Enver_Hoxha" title="Enver Hoxha">Enver Hoxha</a>, leader of Albania</div></div></div>
<p>During World War II, many countries of Central and Eastern Europe had been occupied by the Soviet Union. When the war ended, these countries were incorporated into the Soviet sphere of influence, and the Soviet Union exercised control over their governments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> Soviet occupation of Bulgaria<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStaar198233–34_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStaar198233–34-130">[130]</a></sup> and Poland<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStaar1982156–157_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStaar1982156–157-131">[131]</a></sup> allowed for the immediate establishment of a new government. The Soviet occupation of <a href="/wiki/East_Germany" title="East Germany">East Germany</a> resulted in the creation of a new one-party state controlled by the <a href="/wiki/Socialist_Unity_Party_of_Germany" title="Socialist Unity Party of Germany">Socialist Unity Party of Germany</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStaar1982100–102_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStaar1982100–102-132">[132]</a></sup> The Czechoslovak government was replaced by the <a href="/wiki/Ninth-of-May_Constitution" title="Ninth-of-May Constitution">Ninth-of-May Constitution</a>, which maintained a facade of democracy while the <a href="/wiki/Communist_Party_of_Czechoslovakia" title="Communist Party of Czechoslovakia">Communist Party of Czechoslovakia</a> held authority.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStaar198262–63_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStaar198262–63-133">[133]</a></sup> Following the liberation of Hungary, Communists gained influence in the country by infiltrating its government and carrying out a Communist takeover, implementing a new constitution to implement one party rule.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStaar1982130–131_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStaar1982130–131-134">[134]</a></sup> The Soviet government pressured King <a href="/wiki/Michael_I_of_Romania" title="Michael I of Romania">Michael I of Romania</a> to appoint <a href="/wiki/Petru_Groza" title="Petru Groza">Petru Groza</a> as <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_Romania" title="Prime Minister of Romania">Prime Minister of Romania</a>, and Groza's government enacted a new constitution to implement Communist rule.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStaar1982191–192_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStaar1982191–192-135">[135]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Josip_Broz_Tito" title="Josip Broz Tito">Josip Broz Tito</a> declared a Communist government in Yugoslavia during World War II, which was initially aligned with the Soviet Union. The relations between the countries were strained by Soviet attempts to influence Yugoslavia, leading to the <a href="/wiki/Tito%E2%80%93Stalin_split" title="Tito–Stalin split">Tito–Stalin split</a> in 1948.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStaar1982230–232_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStaar1982230–232-136">[136]</a></sup> Albania was established as a Communist dictatorship under <a href="/wiki/Enver_Hoxha" title="Enver Hoxha">Enver Hoxha</a> in 1944. It was initially aligned with Yugoslavia, but its alignment shifted throughout the Cold War between Yugoslavia, the Soviet Union, and China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStaar19821–2_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStaar19821–2-137">[137]</a></sup> The stability of the Soviet Union weakened in the 1980s. The Soviet economy became unsustainable, and Communist governments lost the support of intellectuals. In 1989, the Soviet Union was dissolved, and Communism was abandoned by the countries of Central and Eastern Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup> Of the new and reformed governments created by the fall of Communism in Europe, only Belarus remained a dictatorship, under the control of <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Lukashenko" title="Alexander Lukashenko">Alexander Lukashenko</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Vladimir_Putin" title="Vladimir Putin">Vladimir Putin</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Russia_under_Vladimir_Putin" title="Russia under Vladimir Putin">unchallenged authoritarian rule</a> over <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a> since 1999 as either <a href="/wiki/President_of_Russia" title="President of Russia">president</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prime_minister_of_Russia" title="Prime minister of Russia">prime minister</a> has also been described as a dictatorship by many political analysts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Putin1_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-Putin1-140">[140]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Putin2_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-Putin2-141">[141]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Putin3_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-Putin3-142">[142]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Latin_America_2">Latin America <span class="anchor" id="Latin_American_dictatorships_of_the_Cold_War"></span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Baby_Doc.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2329" data-file-width="3500" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Baby_Doc.jpg/220px-Baby_Doc.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Baby_Doc.jpg/330px-Baby_Doc.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Baby_Doc.jpg/440px-Baby_Doc.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Baby_Doc.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Jean-Claude_Duvalier" title="Jean-Claude Duvalier">Jean-Claude "Baby Doc" Duvalier</a> succeeded his father <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Duvalier" title="François Duvalier">François "Papa Doc" Duvalier</a> as the ruler of Haiti after his death in 1971.</div></div></div><p>Military dictatorships remained prominent in Latin America during the Cold War, though the number of coups declined starting in the 1980s. Between 1967 and 1991, Haiti and Honduras both underwent three military coups, and Bolivia underwent eight. Argentina, Chile, Ecuador, El Salvador, Guatemala, Panama, Paraguay, Peru, and Uruguay also underwent at least one military coup during the same period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the Caribbean, former President of Cuba <a href="/wiki/Fulgencio_Batista" title="Fulgencio Batista">Fulgencio Batista</a> carried out a <a href="/wiki/1952_Cuban_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="1952 Cuban coup d'état">1952 military coup</a> in Cuba after he determined that he was not going to win the upcoming presidential election. Batista ruled until the <a href="/wiki/Cuban_Revolution" title="Cuban Revolution">Cuban Revolution</a> overthrew his government and established a one-party Marxist state led by <a href="/wiki/Fidel_Castro" title="Fidel Castro">Fidel Castro</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Jean-Claude_Duvalier" title="Jean-Claude Duvalier">Jean-Claude Duvalier</a> consolidated his power as a dictator in Haiti during his presidency in the 1970s, and upon his death in 1971, he passed his position to his son <a href="/wiki/Jean-Claude_Duvalier" title="Jean-Claude Duvalier">Jean-Claude Duvalier</a>, who would rule until 1986.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup>
</p><p>In Central America, <a href="/wiki/Carlos_Castillo_Armas" title="Carlos Castillo Armas">Carlos Castillo Armas</a> took power in Guatemala with a <a href="/wiki/1954_Guatemalan_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="1954 Guatemalan coup d'état">1954 coup</a> with American-trained troops and held power until his assassination in 1957, which in turn resulted in a series of dictatorships during the <a href="/wiki/Guatemalan_Civil_War" title="Guatemalan Civil War">Guatemalan Civil War</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Omar_Torrijos" title="Omar Torrijos">Omar Torrijos</a> took power in Panama with a <a href="/wiki/1968_Panamanian_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="1968 Panamanian coup d'état">1968 military coup</a> until his death in 1981.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup> Several military dictators briefly held power in Panama until <a href="/wiki/Manuel_Noriega" title="Manuel Noriega">Manuel Noriega</a> took power in 1983, but he was deposed by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_invasion_of_Panama" title="United States invasion of Panama">United States invasion of Panama</a> in 1989.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Somoza_family" title="Somoza family">Somoza family</a> ruled Nicaragua either directly or indirectly until they were overthrown during the <a href="/wiki/Nicaraguan_Revolution" title="Nicaraguan Revolution">Nicaraguan Revolution</a> in 1979.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup>
</p><p>In South America, a <a href="/wiki/1948_Venezuelan_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="1948 Venezuelan coup d'état">1948 military coup in Venezuela</a> established the dictatorship of <a href="/wiki/Marcos_P%C3%A9rez_Jim%C3%A9nez" title="Marcos Pérez Jiménez">Marcos Pérez Jiménez</a> who ruled until 1958. <a href="/wiki/Gustavo_Rojas_Pinilla" title="Gustavo Rojas Pinilla">Gustavo Rojas Pinilla</a> took power in a <a href="/wiki/1953_Colombian_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="1953 Colombian coup d'état">1953 coup</a> and ruled until 1957.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Alfredo_Stroessner" title="Alfredo Stroessner">Alfredo Stroessner</a> carried out a <a href="/wiki/1954_Paraguayan_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="1954 Paraguayan coup d'état">1954 military coup</a> in Paraguay that established the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/El_Stronato" title="El Stronato">El Stronato</a></i> military dictatorship until 1989.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/1964_Brazilian_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="1964 Brazilian coup d'état">1964 coup</a> established a <a href="/wiki/Military_dictatorship_in_Brazil" title="Military dictatorship in Brazil">military dictatorship in Brazil</a> that lasted until 1985.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/1968_Peruvian_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="1968 Peruvian coup d'état">1968 military coup</a> established a <a href="/wiki/Revolutionary_Government_of_the_Armed_Forces_of_Peru" title="Revolutionary Government of the Armed Forces of Peru">military dictatorship in Peru</a> until 1980,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup> and in 1992, <a href="/wiki/Alberto_Fujimori" title="Alberto Fujimori">Alberto Fujimori</a> carried out a <a href="/wiki/1992_Peruvian_self-coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="1992 Peruvian self-coup d'état">self-coup in Peru</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hugo_Banzer" title="Hugo Banzer">Hugo Banzer</a> was the military dictator of Bolivia from 1971 to 1978, and he would later go on to be the country's democratically elected president two decades later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-155">[155]</a></sup> In 1972, <a href="/wiki/Guillermo_Rodr%C3%ADguez_(politician)" title="Guillermo Rodríguez (politician)">Guillermo Rodríguez</a> established a dictatorial government in Ecuador and called his government the "Nationalist Revolution".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup> It entered <a href="/wiki/OPEC" title="OPEC">OPEC</a> in 1973 and also imposed agrarian reforms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup> In 1973, <a href="/wiki/Augusto_Pinochet" title="Augusto Pinochet">Augusto Pinochet</a> carried out a <a href="/wiki/1973_Chilean_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="1973 Chilean coup d'état">coup</a> and established a <a href="/wiki/Military_dictatorship_of_Chile_(1973%E2%80%931990)" title="Military dictatorship of Chile (1973–1990)">military dictatorship</a> in Chile that lasted until 1990.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Operation_Condor" title="Operation Condor">Operation Condor</a> was a secret intelligence agreement developed by Chilean dictator <a href="/wiki/Augusto_Pinochet" title="Augusto Pinochet">Augusto Pinochet</a> with his secret police and intelligence organizations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_155-1"><a href="#cite_note-:3-155">[155]</a></sup> It included the governments of military dictatorships in Argentina, Bolivia, Brazil, Chile, Ecuador, Uruguay, Paraguay, and Peru. Under this organization, these governments were able to collaborate with one another in the elimination of political enemies. Operation Condor received tacit and sometimes explicit support from the United States in exchange for support against Communist dictators, and the United States declassified information on Operation Condor after the Cold War.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="21st_century_dictatorships">21st century dictatorships</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Vladimir_Putin_with_Muammar_Gaddafi-2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="334" data-file-width="500" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Vladimir_Putin_with_Muammar_Gaddafi-2.jpg/220px-Vladimir_Putin_with_Muammar_Gaddafi-2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Vladimir_Putin_with_Muammar_Gaddafi-2.jpg/330px-Vladimir_Putin_with_Muammar_Gaddafi-2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Vladimir_Putin_with_Muammar_Gaddafi-2.jpg/440px-Vladimir_Putin_with_Muammar_Gaddafi-2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Vladimir_Putin_with_Muammar_Gaddafi-2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Russian president <a href="/wiki/Vladimir_Putin" title="Vladimir Putin">Vladimir Putin</a> with Libyan leader <a href="/wiki/Muammar_Gaddafi" title="Muammar Gaddafi">Muammar Gaddafi</a> in 2008</div></div></div>
<p>The nature of dictatorship changed in much of the world at the onset of the 21st century. Between the 1990s and the 2000s, most dictators moved away from being "larger-than-life figures" that controlled the populace through terror and isolated themselves from the global community. This was replaced by a trend of developing a positive public image to maintain support among the populace and moderating rhetoric to integrate with the global community.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:6_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-:6-160">[160]</a></sup>
</p><p>Only a small number of one-party states exist in the 21st century. As of 2022, China, Cuba, Laos, North Korea, and Vietnam are Communist one-party states, and Eritrea is a nationalist one-party state. Opposition parties were legalized in Syria and Turkmenistan, but they are still <i>de facto</i> one-party states as of 2022. Other countries with <a href="/wiki/Dominant-party_system" title="Dominant-party system">dominant-party systems</a> also exist. Iraq was a one-party state until the <a href="/wiki/2003_invasion_of_Iraq" title="2003 invasion of Iraq">2003 invasion of Iraq</a>. As of 2022, Brunei, Eswatini, Oman, Saudi Arabia, and Vatican City are absolute monarchies, and the United Arab Emirates is a federation of monarchies. Bhutan was an absolute monarchy until the <a href="/wiki/2008_Bhutanese_National_Assembly_election" title="2008 Bhutanese National Assembly election">2008 Bhutanese National Assembly election</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup> Tonga was an absolute monarchy until the <a href="/wiki/2010_Tongan_general_election" title="2010 Tongan general election">2010 Tongan general election</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup> Belarus under the rule of <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Lukashenko" title="Alexander Lukashenko">Alexander Lukashenko</a> has been described as "the last European dictatorship",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup> though the <a href="/wiki/Russia_under_Vladimir_Putin" title="Russia under Vladimir Putin">rule</a> of <a href="/wiki/Vladimir_Putin" title="Vladimir Putin">Vladimir Putin</a> in Russia has also been described as a dictatorship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Putin1_140-1"><a href="#cite_note-Putin1-140">[140]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Putin2_141-1"><a href="#cite_note-Putin2-141">[141]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Putin3_142-1"><a href="#cite_note-Putin3-142">[142]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Measurement">Measurement</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:261px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Democracy_Index_2020.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1398" data-file-width="2754" decoding="async" height="131" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Democracy_Index_2020.svg/259px-Democracy_Index_2020.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Democracy_Index_2020.svg/389px-Democracy_Index_2020.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Democracy_Index_2020.svg/518px-Democracy_Index_2020.svg.png 2x" width="259"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Democracy_Index_2020.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Democracy_Index" title="Democracy Index">Democracy Index</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Economist_Intelligence_Unit" title="Economist Intelligence Unit">Economist Intelligence Unit</a>, 2020.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup> Green countries are democratic, yellow are <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_regime" title="Hybrid regime">hybrid regimes</a>, and red are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Authoritarian" title="Authoritarian">authoritarian</a> governments.</div></div></div>
<p>One of the tasks in <a href="/wiki/Political_science" title="Political science">political science</a> is to measure and classify regimes as either dictatorships or democracies. <a href="/wiki/Freedom_House" title="Freedom House">Freedom House</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Polity_data_series" title="Polity data series">Polity data series</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Democracy-Dictatorship_Index" title="Democracy-Dictatorship Index">Democracy-Dictatorship Index</a> are three of the most used <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Data_series" title="Data series">data series</a> by political scientists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ClarkGolder2014_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-ClarkGolder2014-166">[166]</a></sup> Generally, two research approaches exist: the minimalist approach, which focuses on whether a country has continued elections that are competitive, and the substantive approach, which expands the concept of democracy to include human rights, <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_the_press" title="Freedom of the press">freedom of the press</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Rule_of_law" title="Rule of law">rule of law</a>. The Democracy-Dictatorship Index is seen as an example of the minimalist approach, whereas the Polity data series is more substantive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AutoJF-3_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-AutoJF-3-167">[167]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MøllerSkaaning2012_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-MøllerSkaaning2012-168">[168]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ClarkGolder2009_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-ClarkGolder2009-169">[169]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AutoJF-4_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-AutoJF-4-170">[170]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economics">Economics</span></h2>
<p>Most dictatorships exist in countries with high levels of poverty. Poverty has a destabilizing effect on government, causing democracy to fail and regimes to fall more often. The form of government does not correlate with the amount of economic growth, and dictatorships on average grow at the same rate as democracies, though dictatorships have been found to have larger fluctuations. Dictators are more likely to implement long-term investments into the country's economy if they feel secure in their power. Exceptions to the pattern of poverty in dictatorships include oil-rich Middle Eastern dictatorships and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/East_Asian_Tiger" title="East Asian Tiger">East Asian Tigers</a> during their periods of dictatorship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEzrowFrantz2011128–131_171-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEzrowFrantz2011128–131-171">[171]</a></sup>
</p><p>The type of economy in a dictatorship can affect how it functions. Economies based on natural resources allow dictators more power, as they can easily <a href="/wiki/Rent-seeking" title="Rent-seeking">extract rents</a> without strengthening or cooperating with other institutions. More complex economies require additional cooperation between the dictator and other groups. The economic focus of a dictatorship often depends on the strength of the opposition, as a weaker opposition allows a dictator to extract additional wealth from the economy through corruption.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-172">[172]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legitimacy_and_stability">Legitimacy and stability</span></h2>
<p>Several factors determine the stability of a dictatorship. They must maintain some degree of popular support to prevent resistance groups from growing. This may be ensured through incentives, such as distribution of financial resources or promises of security, or it may be through repression, in which failing to support the regime is punished. Stability can be weakened when opposition groups grow and unify or when elites are not loyal to the regime.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEzrowFrantz201155–58_173-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEzrowFrantz201155–58-173">[173]</a></sup> One-party dictatorships are generally more stable and last longer than military or personalist dictatorships.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_26-2"><a href="#cite_note-:4-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p>A dictatorship may fall because of a military coup, foreign intervention, negotiation, or popular revolution. A military coup is often carried out when a regime is threatening the country's stability or during periods of societal unrest. Foreign intervention takes place when another country seeks to topple a regime by invading the country or supporting the opposition. A dictator may negotiate the end of a regime if it has lost legitimacy or if a violent removal seems likely. Revolution takes place when the opposition group grows large enough that elites in the regime cannot suppress it or choose not to.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEzrowFrantz201161–67_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEzrowFrantz201161–67-38">[38]</a></sup> Negotiated removals are more likely to end in democracy, while removals by force are more likely to result in a new dictatorial regime. A dictator that has concentrated significant power is more likely to be exiled, imprisoned, or killed after ouster, and accordingly they are more likely to refuse negotiation and cling to power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGeddesWrightFrantz2018206–207_174-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGeddesWrightFrantz2018206–207-174">[174]</a></sup>
</p><p>Dictatorships are typically more aggressive than democracy when in conflict with other nations, as dictators do not have to fear electoral costs of war. Military dictatorships are more prone to conflict due to the inherent military strength associated with such a regime, and personalist dictatorships are more prone to conflict due to the weaker institutions to check the dictator's power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEzrowFrantz2011144–145_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEzrowFrantz2011144–145-175">[175]</a></sup> In the 21st century, dictatorships have moved toward greater integration with the global community and increasingly attempt to present themselves as democratic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:6_160-1"><a href="#cite_note-:6-160">[160]</a></sup> Dictatorships are often recipients of <a href="/wiki/Aid" title="Aid">foreign aid</a> on the condition that they make advances toward democratization.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup> A study found that dictatorships that engage in oil drilling are more likely to remain in power, with 70.63% of the dictators who engage in oil drilling still being in power after 5 years of dictatorship, while only 59.92% of the non-oil producing dictators survive the first 5 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[177]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Elections">Elections</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wahlzettel-3.-Reich.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="345" data-file-width="498" decoding="async" height="152" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Wahlzettel-3.-Reich.jpg/220px-Wahlzettel-3.-Reich.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Wahlzettel-3.-Reich.jpg/330px-Wahlzettel-3.-Reich.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Wahlzettel-3.-Reich.jpg/440px-Wahlzettel-3.-Reich.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wahlzettel-3.-Reich.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An electoral slip in the <a href="/wiki/1936_German_parliamentary_election_and_referendum" title="1936 German parliamentary election and referendum">1936 German parliamentary election</a>. Adolf Hitler and his inner circle are the only option.</div></div></div>
<p>Most dictatorships hold elections to maintain legitimacy and stability, but these elections are typically uncompetitive and the opposition is not permitted to win. Elections allow a dictatorship to exercise some control over the opposition by setting the terms under which the opposition challenges the regime. Elections are also used to control elites within the dictatorship by requiring them to compete with one another and incentivizing them to build support with the populace, allowing the most popular and most competent elites to be promoted in the regime. Elections also support the legitimacy of a dictatorship by presenting the image of a democracy, establishing plausible deniability of its status as a dictatorship for both the populace and foreign governments. Should a dictatorship fail, elections also permit dictators and elites to accept defeat without fearing violent recourse. Dictatorships may influence the results of an election through <a href="/wiki/Electoral_fraud" title="Electoral fraud">electoral fraud</a>, intimidation or bribing of candidates and voters, use of state resources such as media control, manipulation of electoral laws, restricting who may run as a candidate, or disenfranchising demographics that may oppose the dictatorship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEzrowFrantz201167–74_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEzrowFrantz201167–74-178">[178]</a></sup>
</p><p>Before 1990, most dictatorships held elections in which voters could only choose to support the dictatorship. Since the end of the Cold War, more dictatorships have established "semi-competitive" elections in which opposition parties are allowed to participate but they are not allowed to win. This may be done by preventing the opposition from campaigning, banning more popular opposition parties, preventing opposition members from forming a party, or requiring that candidates be a member of the ruling party. Dictatorships may hold semi-competitive elections to qualify for foreign aid or to incentivize the party to expand its information-gathering capacity, particularly at the local level. Semi-competitive elections also have the effect of incentivizing members of the ruling party to provide better treatment of citizens so they will be chosen as party nominees due to their popularity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGeddesWrightFrantz2018137–143_179-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGeddesWrightFrantz2018137–143-179">[179]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Violence">Violence</span></h3>
<p>Violence is used in dictatorships to coerce any opposition to the dictator's rule, often through institutions such as military or police forces. The use of violence by a dictator is often most severe in the first few years of a dictatorship, as the regime has not yet solidified its rule and more detailed information for targeted coercion is not yet available. As the dictatorship becomes more established, it moves away from violence toward other coercive measures, such as restriction of information and tracking of political opposition. Institutions that coerce the opposition through violence may serve different roles or be used to counterbalance one another to prevent one from growing too powerful. <a href="/wiki/Secret_police" title="Secret police">Secret police</a> are used to gather information on and carry out targeted acts of violence against specific political opponents, and <a href="/wiki/Paramilitary" title="Paramilitary">paramilitary</a> forces defend the regime from coups, and formal militaries defend the dictatorship during foreign invasion and major civil conflicts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGeddesWrightFrantz2018154–167_180-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGeddesWrightFrantz2018154–167-180">[180]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Terrorism" title="Terrorism">Terrorism</a> is less common in dictatorships. Allowing the opposition to have representation in the regime, such as through a legislature, further reduces the likelihood of terrorist attacks in a dictatorship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[181]</a></sup> Military and one-party dictatorships are more likely to experience terrorism than personalist dictatorships, as these regimes are under more pressure to undergo institutional change in response to terrorism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[182]</a></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite_note-ClarkGolder2009-169"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-ClarkGolder2009_169-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFClarkGolderGolder2009">Clark, William Roberts; Golder, Matt; Golder, Sona Nadenichek (2009). <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=RmPuAAAAMAAJ" rel="nofollow"><i>Principles of comparative politics</i></a>. CQ Press. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-87289-289-7" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-87289-289-7"><bdi>978-0-87289-289-7</bdi></a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Principles+of+comparative+politics&amp;rft.pub=CQ+Press.&amp;rft.date=2009&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-87289-289-7&amp;rft.aulast=Clark&amp;rft.aufirst=William+Roberts&amp;rft.au=Golder%2C+Matt&amp;rft.au=Golder%2C+Sona+Nadenichek&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DRmPuAAAAMAAJ&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ADictatorship"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-AutoJF-4-170"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-AutoJF-4_170-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a class="external text" href="http://jcr.sagepub.com/content/early/2012/10/12/0022002712459707?patientinform-links=yes&amp;legid=spjcr;0022002712459707v1" rel="nofollow">Divergent Incentives for Dictators: Domestic Institutions and (International Promises Not to) Torture</a> <a class="external text" href="http://faculty.ucmerced.edu/cconrad2/Academic/Research_files/JCRAppendix25March2011.pdf" rel="nofollow">Appendix</a> "Unlike substantive measures of democracy (e.g., Polity IV and Freedom House), the binary conceptualization of democracy most recently described by Cheibub, Gandhi and Vree-land (2010) focuses on one institution—elections—to distinguish between dictatorships and democracies. Using a minimalist measure of democracy rather than a substantive one better allows for the isolation of causal mechanisms (Cheibub, Gandhi and Vreeland, 2010, 73) linking regime type to human rights outcomes."</span>
</li>
